       <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/" title="Permalink to NPM &amp; left-pad: Have We Forgotten How To Program?" rel="bookmark" class="date-link"> <span class="post-date"> March 23, 2016 <span class="bg">&nbsp;</span> </span> </a> 
       <ul> 
        <li class="comments"><a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comments">316 Comments</a></li>

      <div class="middle col-left clearfix"> 
       <h1 class="title"> NPM &amp; left-pad: Have We Forgotten How To Program? </h1> 
       <div class="entry clearfix"> 
        <p>Okay developers, time to have a serious talk. As you are probably already aware, this week React, Babel, and a bunch of other high-profile packages on NPM broke. The reason they broke is rather astounding:</p> 
        <p>A simple NPM package called <a href="https://www.npmjs.com/package/left-pad">left-pad</a> that was a dependency of their code.</p> 
        <p>left-pad, at the time of writing this, <a href="https://github.com/azer/left-pad">has 11 stars on GitHub</a>. The entire package is <a href="https://github.com/azer/left-pad/blob/master/index.js">11 simple lines that implement a basic left-pad string function</a>. In case those links ever die, here is the entire code of the left-pad package:</p> 
        <pre class="brush: jscript; title: ; notranslate" title="">
module.exports = leftpad;
function leftpad (str, len, ch) {
  str = String(str);
  var i = -1;
  if (!ch &amp;&amp; ch !== 0) ch = ' ';
  len = len - str.length;
  while (++i &lt; len) {
    str = ch + str;
  }
  return str;
}
</pre> 
        <p>What concerns me here is that <i>so many packages and projects</i> took on a <b>dependency</b> for a simple left padding string function, rather than their developers taking 2 minutes to write such a basic function themselves.</p> 
        <p>As a result of learning about the left-pad disaster, I started investigating the NPM ecosystem. Here are some of the things that I observed:</p> 
        <ul> 
         <li>There’s a package called <b><a href="https://www.npmjs.com/package/isarray">isArray</a></b> that has 880,000 downloads a day, and 18 million downloads in February of 2016. It has <b>72 dependent NPM packages</b>. Here’s its <b>entire 1 line of code</b>: <pre class="brush: jscript; title: ; notranslate" title="">return toString.call(arr) == '[object Array]';</pre> </li> 
         <li>There’s a package called <b><a href="https://www.npmjs.com/package/is-positive-integer">is-positive-integer</a></b> (<a href="https://github.com/tjmehta/is-positive-integer">GitHub</a>) that is <b><a href="https://github.com/tjmehta/is-positive-integer/blob/master/index.js">4 lines long</a></b> and as of yesterday required <b>3 dependencies</b> to use. The author has since refactored it to require 0 dependencies, but I have to wonder why it wasn’t that way in the first place.</li> 
         <li>A fresh install of the <b><a href="https://www.npmjs.com/package/babel">Babel</a></b> package includes <b>41,000 files</b></li> 
         <li>A <b>blank jspm/npm-based app template</b> now starts with <b>28,000+ files</b></li> 
        </ul> 
        <p>All of this leads me to wonder…</p> 
        <h2>Have We Forgotten How To Program?</h2> 
        <p>On what possible plane of existence is this a better solution to past problems? How are hundreds of dependencies and 28,000 files for a blank project template anything but overly complicated and insane?</p> 
        <p>I get the impression that the NPM ecosystem participants have created a penchant for micro-packages. Rather than write any functions or code, it seems that they prefer to depend on something that someone else has written. It feels to me as if the entire job of an NPM-participating developer is writing the smallest amount of code possible to string existing library calls together in order to create something new that functions uniquely for their personal or business need.</p> 
        <h2>Functions Are Not Packages</h2> 
        <p>Functions are too small to make into a package and dependency. Pure functions don’t have cohesion; they are random snippets of code and nothing more. Who really wants a “cosine” dependency? We’d all really like a “trigonometry” dependency instead which encompasses many “tricky” functions that we don’t want to have to write ourselves. This is much more akin to how .NET and other frameworks create a “core” library of basic functionality. Such a library is vetted by the creators of the language and pretty much guaranteed to be correct and bug-free.</p> 
        <h2>Third Party Problems</h2> 
        <p>There’s absolutely no guarantee that what someone else has written is correct, or even works well. Even if correct, is it the most optimal solution possible? At least when you write the code yourself, you can easily modify it to fix bugs and improve its efficiency. Not that there should be many bugs in 1 line functions.</p> 
        <p>Second, even if the package’s logic is correct, I can’t help but be amazed by the fact that developers are taking on dependencies for single line functions that they should be able to write with their eyes closed. In my opinion, if you cannot write a left-pad, is-positive-integer, or isArray function in 5 minutes flat (including the time you spend Googling), then <b>you don’t actually know how to code</b>. Any of these would make a great code screening interview question to determine whether or not a candidate can code.</p> 
        <p>Finally, stringing APIs together and calling it programming doesn’t make it programming. It’s some crazy form of dependency hacking that involves the cloud, over-engineering things, and complexity far beyond what’s actually needed to create great applications.</p> 
        <p>What’s worse is that if any of your code (or the 3rd party dependency code) has a bug or breaks, you won’t know how to debug or fix it if you don’t know how to program.</p> 
        <h2>Strive For Few Dependencies</h2> 
        <p>Every package that you use adds yet another <b>dependency</b> to your project. Dependencies, by their very name, are things you <b>need</b> in order for your code to function. The more dependencies you take on, the more points of failure you have. Not to mention the more chance for error: have you vetted any of the programmers who have written these functions that you depend on daily?</p> 
        <p>Take on a dependency for any complex functionality that would take a lot of time, money, and/or debugging to write yourself. Things like a database access layer (ORM) or caching client should be dependencies because they’re complicated and the risk of the dependency is well worth the savings and efficiency.</p> 
        <p>But, for the love of all that is programming, write your own bloody basic programming functions. Taking on dependencies for these one-liners is just nuts. Don’t believe me? Just ask the React team how well their week has been going, and whether they wish they had written those 11 lines for left-padding a string themselves.</p> 
        <p><i>Follow David Haney on Twitter at <a href="http://www.twitter.com/@haneycodes">@haneycodes</a></i></p> 
        <p><i>Updated Jan 13 2017 with some minor grammar and sentence structure changes.</i></p> 
       </div>

              <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127268"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T23:15:16+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 11:15 pm</time></a> </span> 
              <span class="edit"></span> 
              <p>Actually, after reading the link from Luis, I draw the same conclusion as David. Where is the complexity? Just write it yourself.</p> 
              <p>Seen the same breakage situation happen all over the npm ecosystem with regards to another package called rimraf. I guess it is just too hard for most to remove a file, or tree of files, from the file-system using the plain old node API. Just write the function yourself.</p> 
              <p>If you must, make it your own npm package, and depend on it. Strive for few external dependencies.</p>

             <li class="comment odd alt depth-4" id="li-comment-127314"> <a name="comment-127314"></a> 
              <div class="comment-container"> 
               <div class="avatar">
                <img alt="" src="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/ee2a5efb85a0f3f18bdfd7345c1d1d98?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/ee2a5efb85a0f3f18bdfd7345c1d1d98?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
               </div> 
               <div id="comment-127314" class="comment-content"> 
                <span class="name"><a href="https://phptrends.com/" rel="external nofollow" class="url">Slava Vishnyakov</a> <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
                <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127314"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T05:18:03+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 5:18 am</time></a> </span> 
                <span class="edit"></span> 
                <p>The complexity is in the all of the off-by-one bugs and forgotten cases. The same can be said about fro ex. HTTP client. What’s the big deal, it’s just “GET / HTTP/1.0\r\n\r\n” – then you understand you need headers, then encodings, then non-conforming servers, then uploads, etc.. </p> 
                <p>In left pad you see things like var i = -1 which probably started life as var i =0 and a off-by-one bug. </p> 
                <p>return toString.call(arr) == ‘[object Array]’;</p> 
                <p>You probably need to console.log it first. Also as soon as you log it – you start to think – is it a correct way? Etc.. It’s just not worth the programmers time to debug all those minor things. That’s why libraries exist, that’s why standard libraries exists – so that millions of programmer don’t waste their time writing (and debugging!) the same stupid thing.</p> 
                <div class="reply"> 
                 <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127314#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127314&quot;, &quot;127314&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Slava Vishnyakov">Reply</a>

                 <div id="comment-127374" class="comment-content"> 
                  <span class="name">Ben <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
                  <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127374"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T10:59:26+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 10:59 am</time></a> </span> 
                  <span class="edit"></span> 
                  <p>A voice of reason.</p> 
                  <div class="reply">

               <li class="comment odd alt depth-5" id="li-comment-127408"> <a name="comment-127408"></a> 
                <div class="comment-container"> 
                 <div class="avatar">
                  <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/c3985dffcdc646b67ed0699a364639e2?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/c3985dffcdc646b67ed0699a364639e2?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
                 </div> 
                 <div id="comment-127408" class="comment-content"> 
                  <span class="name">Wombat <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
                  <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127408"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T15:48:45+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 3:48 pm</time></a> </span> 
                  <span class="edit"></span> 
                  <p>Lines of code is not a good metric. If you make a really efficient random number generator function it might make sense to package it as a module even though it might only be a few lines of code. That said, there is a difference between an HTTP client and isArray. HTTP is genuinely complex and if all you need is to grab a resource from a URL you really don’t need to know the intricacies of HTTP protocol. The scope of an HTTP client library is well defined and actually hides complexity that most developers will never need to access. isArray is a different case. It is not actually hiding any complexity. If you are going to develop things in javascript you need to know how to deal with arrays as well as other default object types. If you don’t know, you need to find out. Otherwise it will eventually bite you in the ass. In this instance, isArray actually depends on string representation of an array object to stay the same. And I don’t think [object Array] string is part of any standard, so the next implementation of JS might do things differently. The library is not hiding complexity, it is hiding important implementation details.</p> 
                  <div class="reply"> 
                  </div>
                  <!-- /.reply --> 
                 </div>

                </div>

               <li class="comment even depth-5" id="li-comment-127466"> <a name="comment-127466"></a>

                 </div> 
                 <div id="comment-127466" class="comment-content"> 
                  <span class="name">Ivan <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
                  <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127466"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-25T08:56:27+00:00">March 25, 2016 at 8:56 am</time></a> </span> 
                  <span class="edit"></span> 
                  <p>This. When our devs pull low level modules (nothing that is 1 line!), its not because they cannot code, but because their head space is focused on making our actual project something amazing. I don’t want my devs spending time recreating something that has been solved.</p> 
                  <p>Totally agree that there are some ridiculous packages out there, but I think there is a legit use case to pull small libraries and low level tools, its part of the foundation of the progress of programming to be able to abstract up from whats already been solved to spend your time solving larger problems.</p>

                <span class="edit"></span> 
                <p>Yeah! Just write your own function for deleting files!<br> That supports recursive deletion of infinitely complex trees.<br> And globbing patterns.<br> And intelligent error handling.<br> And automatic retries after configurable timeouts.<br> And special error handling for Windows systems.<br> And is usable as a library and stand-alone.</p> 
                <p>It’s not that hard – it took 17 contributors only 5 years, after all. ?</p> 
                <div class="reply"> 
                 <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127450#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127450&quot;, &quot;127450&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to bid">Reply</a> 
                </div>

              <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-131193"><time pubdate datetime="2016-10-27T12:56:58+00:00">October 27, 2016 at 12:56 pm</time></a> </span> 
              <span class="edit"></span> 
              <p>” misunderstanding the npm ecosystem and small module philosophy”</p> 
              <p>I think the philosophy is invalid. And you know what? NPM itself is the proof.</p> 
              <p>The most popular and useful packages on NPM are not ‘little blocks that do 1 thing and 1 thing well’. They are proper modules. They do a bunch of highly related things, and they do it well. </p> 
              <p>The best Node modules are those that ‘could have been written’ by a small team of solid Engineers working for ABC corp. But they just happen to be giving it away.</p>

         <li class="comment odd alt depth-2" id="li-comment-127252"> <a name="comment-127252"></a> 
          <div class="comment-container"> 
           <div class="avatar">
            <img alt="" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/429bd794185772fb4626b5a4b1b60167?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/429bd794185772fb4626b5a4b1b60167?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
           </div> 
           <div id="comment-127252" class="comment-content"> 
            <span class="name">Jim <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
            <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127252"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T21:02:06+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 9:02 pm</time></a> </span> 
            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>Prior to the emergence of jQuery, JavaScript development was mess. Polyfills for different browsers and other abominations all copy-pasted from project to project. It was a toy language that could be hacked into doing cool things (Google Maps and Gmail). The idea that a layer of abstraction that could hide browser complexity was a revelation. This idea took hold and grew till we got to the sitation where we are now. Simultaneously the “one language to rule them all” cargo cult and SPA trends emerged and JS ended up being in the right place, at the right time with the right toolsets.</p> 
            <p>Any edge cases and incompatible language implementations -including incorrect or missing operators(!) can be abstracted away and incorporated into the package and shared. I personally think that modules are a good way of working around the rickety mess that is JavaScript development. Perhaps the real answer it to stop fetishising a 20-year-old language that was thrown together in a week, and make a concerted effort to standardize on something better.</p> 
            <div class="reply"> 
             <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127252#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127252&quot;, &quot;127252&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Jim">Reply</a>

         <li class="comment odd alt depth-2" id="li-comment-127278"> <a name="comment-127278"></a> 
          <div class="comment-container"> 
           <div class="avatar">
            <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/cdf4c5755259596b5b52f37b5c8afa46?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/cdf4c5755259596b5b52f37b5c8afa46?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
           </div> 
           <div id="comment-127278" class="comment-content"> 
            <span class="name">Chris <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
            <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127278"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T00:25:52+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 12:25 am</time></a> </span> 
            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>There’s a cognitive dissonance going on in this community that I just can’t wrap my head around. I get the impression that all this “hiding complexity” is side talk for “someone else on my team’s problem”. This doesn’t even pass the simplest logical sniff test test, people. I like Sindre. He makes cool stuff, but man…</p> 
            <p>“Imagine if PC manufacturers all made their own CPUs. Most would do it badly. The computer would be more expensive and we would have slower innovation. Instead most use Intel, ARM, etc.”</p> 
            <p>What in holy hell is he talking about? This is such a broken analogy. A CPU is a self-contained unit that can be plugged in all sorts of systems and has value on its own — but it’s made up of many different functions that a single team has worked on and developed and tested and is totally vested in delivering the best CPU to market.</p> 
            <p>A better analogy would be to compare some engineering team designing a CPU and deciding to break it apart into 10,000 micro components and handing them all off to PC manufacturers. </p> 
            <p>“You wanted to buy a CPU from us? That’s not how we do business. We found it was much quicker to push out features to you if we went for a more lego block approach. Instead of a CPU you *get* to choose a combination of any of our 1,000 micro components. Each one is on a different release cycle to ensure velocity and it’s up to you to ensure the versions you are using are compatible. Oh. We also found is was better to outsource our 1,000 components to 100 different short-term contractors across the world. The contract terms? Yeah. We let our contractors terminate the relationship at any time and we don’t ensure any sort of QA. They’re all great teams, don’t worry about it! Also, none of these teams are expected to know what the other teams are working on or what changes they are developing. We feel that organizing at that level slows down innovation. Almost forgot! You also get to solder everything together yourself and how you see fit — we supply you the soldering board and the solder at no additional charge!”</p> 
            <p>Does this seem like a sane world and a company you’d want to do business with? Maybe. I sure as shit wouldn’t. Is this a symptom of open source? Maybe. But there are tons of projects and ecosystems out there that are open source and don’t operate this way and don’t get the kind of slack this community gets.</p> 
            <div class="reply"> 
             <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127278#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127278&quot;, &quot;127278&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Chris">Reply</a> 
            </div>
            <!-- /.reply --> 
           </div>

           <!-- #comment-## --> 
           <li class="comment odd alt depth-3" id="li-comment-127365"> <a name="comment-127365"></a> 
            <div class="comment-container"> 
             <div class="avatar">
              <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/9a3e3b376eb1c63a9e12a5adb31b8291?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/9a3e3b376eb1c63a9e12a5adb31b8291?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
             </div> 
             <div id="comment-127365" class="comment-content"> 
              <span class="name">Joey <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
              <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127365"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T10:23:42+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 10:23 am</time></a> </span> 
              <span class="edit"></span> 
              <p>^^^ THIS. Reading that response only validated for me exactly why Node package ecosystem is broken. He mentioned that small code snippets are worth importing, but I’d rather not import a package that includes finding a Windows user’s home directory if I only ever need to run on Linux. It’s that kind of thoughtless, careless programming that leads to bloat and unnecessary complexity.</p> 
              <p>Look at the way “node_modules” becomes a relative dependency of each dependency. Stating it that way in plain English makes it clear that this is a potential recursion problem. Specifically, a filesystem recursion problem. Go ahead and Google for “npm windows character limit”. It’s the inevitable outcome of such a design. Or look at the following post:</p> 
              <p><a href="http://www.jagregory.com/writings/docker-container-out-of-space/" rel="nofollow">http://www.jagregory.com/writings/docker-container-out-of-space/</a></p> 
              <p>That’s what happens when you hit the inode limit on Linux filesystems. And you’ll notice that the only place this ever happens is in the npm ecosystem. Directory limits, open file descriptor limits, pathname limits… all npm.</p> 
              <p>The idea that your project might feasibly encounter an OS or filesystem limit by simply installing its own dependencies is absurd. I agree wholeheartedly with the OP; programming today (at least in JS world) involves almost as much work choosing what 3rd parties to depend on as it does writing and thinking about your own code. Being lazy used to be a great quality in a programmer — it typically translated into being extra efficient and ensuring that you wouldn’t be replicating your efforts on the same logic over and over again. Now, it seems to have produced a generation of programmers who believe it’s easier to import someone else’s snippet, no matter how bloated and how many unnecessary genericisms have been introduced in order to make it publicly consumable, than to think thoroughly about your own code and treat complexity as the enemy that it is.</p> 
              <p>Looking at that left-pad module makes me cringe.</p> 
              <div class="reply">

         <li class="comment even depth-2" id="li-comment-127528"> <a name="comment-127528"></a> 
          <div class="comment-container"> 
           <div class="avatar">
            <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/c8f89b515ca541a30d6e622ea90f970b?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/c8f89b515ca541a30d6e622ea90f970b?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
           </div> 
           <div id="comment-127528" class="comment-content"> 
            <span class="name">mirfilip <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
            <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127528"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-27T06:10:45+00:00">March 27, 2016 at 6:10 am</time></a> </span> 
            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>I gave myself couple of days since this blogpost was written to digest the community reaction and the comments here. It’s been around two years since I stopped coding in JS myself but I still follow the ecosystem. I think this gives me a good position to comment on.</p> 
            <p>David’s examples and conclusions may be a bit overdrawn but they indeed show there are some fundamentally wrong things with JS ecosystem nowadays.</p> 
            <p>Granted, people defending “make a dependency of everything” approach had some valid points too. Here are my thoughts:<br> * Literally no-one in their sane mind would copy&amp;paste the solutions to every problem just because they wrote it themselves. If you do, you’re a programmer waiting to be proven wrong. Making your solution public, scrutinizing, testing against “yet another” edge case no one thought about before is a great approach. This statement is language agnostic, really. If you stick to your holy solution, promote that to become a part of your “standard library” and have it peer reviewed. All these people that commented for copying – they also forgot how to program, cause DRY principle is known and widely accepted for some 20 years or more.<br> * Yes, it could happen to bigger packages. Removal of left-pad is NOT to be blamed. But still… Take the opportunity to think about the problems NPM has. And no, “it has 50k downloads, it has to be awesome” is not an argument. If any of the bigger packages depended upon this left-pad, with all the problems it has (just pointed out by blogpost comments alone), it’s really weak. You’ve got to wonder what the criteria of validating the dependencies are for maintainers of these frameworks. You think such mistakes happen to JS only? No. I can name examples from Ruby, Python, Java and PHP. The difference is the mentioned languages had their “big packages” stick around for long enough to have been vetted and improved. You don’t have time for that because of constant churn… which segways me to another point.<br> * I once read <a href="https://jsinsights.com/why-javascript-churn-is-a-good-thing-607b0d53018c" rel="nofollow">https://jsinsights.com/why-javascript-churn-is-a-good-thing-607b0d53018c</a> which left me totally dumbfounded. How do you guys cope with the situation that the technology/state of the art approach/framework that you started your project with, is becoming “not cool anymore” by the time you finish it? No, “life fast, die young” doesn’t apply here. It’s fine for homemade projects. It’s not for “close-to-enterprise” level solutions. The just released big project written in AngularJS 1.x is not cool anymore because everyone does it React way.<br> * Everyone agreed the modules like “isArray” or “isPositiveInteger” are bad and should be a part of some kind of “standard library” of the ECMA. I remember the sad times before jQuery and the fresh breeze it brought. That was because everyone was sick and tired of handling all the quirks themselves and this tool solved things. Don’t forget, JS was still a frontend, browser based scripting language. You could argue, sticking with a bad language “patched” by such a good utility library was the best thing community had. The problem is, since then, JavaScript started to become a backend language (NodeJS) with almost no improvements to the “stdlib” (yes, I’m aware of ES6). Don’t get me wrong, I’m the last person to push NodeJS out of the backend party, but doesn’t it give you red alerts when you start your next project based on that? A language suited for one thing is not necessarily a good candidate for another. Remember Java and applets?</p> 
            <p>So, having expressed my doubts on the _current_ state of things, I cheer JS community to grow out of child age problems and move on.</p> 
            <div class="reply"> 
             <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127528#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127528&quot;, &quot;127528&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to mirfilip">Reply</a> 
            </div>

       <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-127226"> <a name="comment-127226"></a> 
        <div class="comment-container"> 
         <div class="avatar">
          <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/f54695449d1280b074b4aea6366c3297?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/f54695449d1280b074b4aea6366c3297?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
         </div> 
         <div id="comment-127226" class="comment-content"> 
          <span class="name">Noah Yetter <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
          <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127226"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T19:20:55+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 7:20 pm</time></a> </span> 
          <span class="edit"></span> 
          <p>I think you’re pointing to the right symptom and drawing the wrong conclusion.</p> 
          <p>These packages proliferate because JavaScript’s standard library is so woefully inadequate. It’s ridiculous that anyone has to write their own left-pad function in the first place. The problem isn’t that people “don’t know how to code”, it’s that they’re operating in an environment where basic functionality has to be implemented by end-users (i.e. developers), and where it’s — perhaps reasonably — standard operating procedure to import someone else’s solution rather than write your own.</p> 
          <p>And it’s not just JS, there are plenty of examples in other ecosystems. In Java there’s no reason to write your own StringUtils.isNullOrEmpty() when you can just import Apache Commons or Google Guava. That said, it’s embarrassing that such a commonly-needed thing isn’t in the JDK itself.</p> 
          <div class="reply"> 
           <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127226#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127226&quot;, &quot;127226&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Noah Yetter">Reply</a>

               <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127399#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127399&quot;, &quot;127399&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Beeen">Reply</a>

             <li class="comment even depth-4" id="li-comment-127443"> <a name="comment-127443"></a> 
              <div class="comment-container"> 
               <div class="avatar">
                <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/f6b6bbed6fc8c12bff1ab70b61db6a0a?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/f6b6bbed6fc8c12bff1ab70b61db6a0a?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
               </div> 
               <div id="comment-127443" class="comment-content"> 
                <span class="name"><a href="http://www.augur.io" rel="external nofollow" class="url">Vince</a> <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
                <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127443"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T22:52:17+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 10:52 pm</time></a> </span> 
                <span class="edit"></span> 
                <p>I agree with this fellow. He probably does know how to program. But I don’t, and I use npm. I’m probably 19 and leaking credentials on Github. Or maybe I’m 45 and Node is interesting to me after a career in ________. Or maybe I’m 32 and I work in QA. You just never know. The level of skills required for different requirements within different size teams is so varied. Supposedly JavaScript was supposed to be somewhat accessible, and we aren’t shoving the entirety of ‘programming’ down the development pipe… which is one reason some people distinguish between ‘programming’ and ‘coding.’ It also may be why some people are and some people are ‘junior developers.’ I’d say creativity is a sign that mastery is emerging.</p>

                <span class="name">Chuck W. <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
                <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127274"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T23:50:18+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 11:50 pm</time></a> </span> 
                <span class="edit"></span> 
                <p>They may or may not be able to code, but they sure can stitch together hundreds of disparate “modules” to build a more or less functional, if not provably so, system. It’s about saving time by not having to remember the language’s idiosyncrasies or belting out a few lines of code for a function or maintaining a local repository if something critical to your project disappears.</p> 
                <p>Do the developers who live in this echosystem (deliberate spelling), unit test any of these functions to ensure they an handle all edge cases, or at least all the edge cases that might occur in their project? I’ve seen too many questionable code snippets in the comments and blog posts by others in responding to this hot mess to have any faith this is being done.</p>

            <p>Totally agree.</p> 
            <p>If you grab a CS professor who definitely knows how to program but isn’t an expert in JS, and ask her to write isArray, she would either just write instanceof Array, introducing a bug without noticing, or she would have no idea and search everywhere on the internet, find multiple solutions, and read through all the comments trying to decide which one looks most reliable.</p>

         <li class="comment odd alt depth-2" id="li-comment-127310"> <a name="comment-127310"></a> 
          <div class="comment-container"> 
           <div class="avatar">
            <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/30d1f51adeaff7626fe9e00c3873b65f?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/30d1f51adeaff7626fe9e00c3873b65f?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
           </div> 
           <div id="comment-127310" class="comment-content"> 
            <span class="name">RandomStranger <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
            <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127310"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T04:40:56+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 4:40 am</time></a> </span> 
            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>Your comparison is utterly ludicrous. Apache Commons is a full-flexed library, with lots of useful functions in a single package. It’s pretty much exactly the *opposite* of having to include “Apache CheckStringForNullOrEmpty” to add a single useful function to your project, and then having to include “Apache LeftPadString” to get another, and each of them pulling in a dozen more dependencies for code that you could just write yourself in just a handful of lines. And the fact that you can’t see the problem with that is what proves the original point of the article.</p> 
            <div class="reply"> 
             <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127310#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127310&quot;, &quot;127310&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to RandomStranger">Reply</a>

          <div class="comment-container"> 
           <div class="avatar">
            <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/f34900f536ee6bb7e3635455db3857ea?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/f34900f536ee6bb7e3635455db3857ea?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">

           <div id="comment-127336" class="comment-content"> 
            <span class="name">Rolf <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
            <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127336"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T07:00:52+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 7:00 am</time></a> </span> 
            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>&gt; In Java there’s no reason to write your own StringUtils.isNullOrEmpty() when you can just import Apache Commons or Google Guava.<br> But why the fuck do I need to import some huge package for a simple ((string == NULL) || (string.length() == 0)) ? Stuff like JDBC, yes. But that? Seriously?</p>

             </div> 
             <div id="comment-127383" class="comment-content"> 
              <span class="name">Sammy <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
              <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127383"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T11:41:30+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 11:41 am</time></a> </span> 
              <span class="edit"></span> 
              <p>This is actually a perfect example of the problem at hand. Look at your code again.</p> 
              <p>If the string is NULL you’re going to get an NPE when your code tries to run string.length()</p> 
              <p>The correct method would be:</p> 
              <p>if (string != null &amp;&amp; string.length() == 0)</p> 
              <p>But otherwise you’re right. You don’t import a massive package for a single use, that’s silly. You import it to provide a series of useful quick functions to improve legibility. If it’s a one time thing? Just copy someone’s solution from online, don’t import and entire package.</p>

                  <span class="name">Nerot <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
                  <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127400"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T14:17:49+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 2:17 pm</time></a> </span> 
                  <span class="edit"></span> 
                  <p>If the string is null, the string.length() will never run, as you only need one condition to be true in an || statement for the language to just jump right into the code block.</p> 
                  <p>whereas in your code, if the string is null the language will skip the following code block altogether, which is not the desired behavior of an isNullOrEmpty function.</p>

       <li class="comment odd alt thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-127227"> <a name="comment-127227"></a> 
        <div class="comment-container"> 
         <div class="avatar">
          <img alt="" src="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/e6f5715380c85aa1b9d5bc5e9fc89891?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/e6f5715380c85aa1b9d5bc5e9fc89891?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
         </div> 
         <div id="comment-127227" class="comment-content"> 
          <span class="name"><a href="http://ilikekillnerds.com" rel="external nofollow" class="url">Dwayne Charrington</a> <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
          <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127227"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T19:27:27+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 7:27 pm</time></a> </span> 
          <span class="edit"></span> 
          <p>Spot on, David.</p> 
          <p>This is the one thing that really concerns me about front-end development as a whole. We have become so dependent on tooling and package managers, we don’t realise how weak the foundations of the ecosystem actually are. The entire front-end development ecosystem is one massive house of cards, pull out one of the cards and it all falls apart.</p> 
          <p>I am honestly surprised that something like this did not happen sooner to be honest. If developers can’t write their own left padding implementations, maybe the end of mankind is going to happen sooner rather than later. You point out there are packages for isArray and other numerous basic tasks that require very little code to implement. We’ve gone package mad. There are thousands of packages with just a few lines of code, who is using them nobody knows.</p> 
          <p>The flaw with Npm is that it encourages this micro package approach so much, you look at packages like Babel and it does your head in tracing all of the dependencies. One dependency sometimes has upwards of 30 or 40 dependencies, but guess what, most likely each of those dependencies has their own dependencies as well. It can really get quite perplexing, once you go down the rabbit hole you never truly reach the bottom when it comes to Npm packages. One package can possibly have hundreds of dependencies, no wonder Npm is slow.</p> 
          <p>There is a meme here. </p> 
          <p>“Yo dawg, I heard you like Npm packages, so we put an Npm package in your Npm package, so you can install while you install.”</p> 
          <div class="reply"> 
           <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127227#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127227&quot;, &quot;127227&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Dwayne Charrington">Reply</a> 
          </div>

           </div> 
           <div id="comment-127261" class="comment-content"> 
            <span class="name">Justin <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
            <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127261"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T22:14:39+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 10:14 pm</time></a> </span> 
            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>But this itself is an illustration of the problem with the micro-dependency philosophy that’s best summed up by the phrase “house of cards”: all the damage done by the kik/unpublishing issue is due to the hidden dependencies inherent in the npm ecosystem. Major projects were taken down without ever choosing that dependency or even being aware of it. AAA projects are built on opaque foundations. If the leading JS libs today don’t even have a grasp of their dependencies, how is any less-rigourous, less-engineered project supposed to confidence in its stability or quality?</p> 
            <div class="reply">

       <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-127229"> <a name="comment-127229"></a> 
        <div class="comment-container"> 
         <div class="avatar">
          <img alt="" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/adf5c542fe6589f7283c884394281e36?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/adf5c542fe6589f7283c884394281e36?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
         </div> 
         <div id="comment-127229" class="comment-content"> 
          <span class="name"><a href="http://codefoundries.com" rel="external nofollow" class="url">Aleksandar Chalakov</a> <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
          <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127229"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T19:31:40+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 7:31 pm</time></a> </span> 
          <span class="edit"></span> 
          <p>One advantage of micro packages is that if a certain piece of code is used in different packages, there is only one implementation of it sent to the client, instead every module re-implementing the same function thus increasing the client code footprint. That said, the price we pay for this is high. What if a hacker takes control of the NPM account responsible for one of these one liners? The possibilities are endless.<br> Hopefully this un-publishing incident will serve as a wake up call.</p> 
          <div class="reply"> 
           <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127229#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127229&quot;, &quot;127229&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Aleksandar Chalakov">Reply</a>

           <li class="comment odd alt depth-3" id="li-comment-127567"> <a name="comment-127567"></a> 
            <div class="comment-container"> 
             <div class="avatar">
              <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/ce3d25f7e76ed7a9f8e8c398183a4371?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/ce3d25f7e76ed7a9f8e8c398183a4371?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
             </div> 
             <div id="comment-127567" class="comment-content"> 
              <span class="name">Thomas <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
              <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127567"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-28T09:59:31+00:00">March 28, 2016 at 9:59 am</time></a> </span> 
              <span class="edit"></span> 
              <p>pad-left is no more efficient than String.repeat (ECMA6) + String concatenation at actually producing a padded string (it’s actually quite a bit slower). What it does is depend on a library (repeat-string, from the same author) that shamelessly cheats at benchmarks by caching the last result and input string in the parent namespace. The benchmarks are then ran using the same input, meaning it always pulls from the cached result and never has to modify it. The result being that it appears to be very fast when you run benchmarks, but if you ever use a different pad character, it will clear the cache, meaning it actually has to do the work of generating the string again. If you’re generating line numbers, then this can actually be somewhat useful, but if you have String.repeat already (which you do), then just use it. It’s a one liner. A one liner that could have avoided this mess. Even if you don’t have String.repeat, replacing it without cheating and getting near String.repeat speeds is trivial.</p> 
              <div class="reply"> 
               <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127567#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127567&quot;, &quot;127567&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Thomas">Reply</a> 
              </div>

            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>Not so sure about the `only one implementation of it sent to the client` part. </p> 
            <p>With npm2 I’m pretty sure I would get a separate copy of left-pad.js for every dependency in the project that has a dependency on left-pad.</p> 
            <p>But we’re all in npm3 stage now, right?</p> 
            <p>Right. The thing is: I still am not aware of what dependencies my dependencies have. I wasn’t aware I used left-pad every day in my work. I might have even written my own left padding function or, even worse, add another dependency to another module with same functionality. Then I’d have two pieces of code, doing the same, but implemented in two different ways, and both send to client.</p>

          <div class="comment-container"> 
           <div class="avatar">
            <img alt="" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/4f5c1ff6b23a6fb493b4bf6559418247?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/4f5c1ff6b23a6fb493b4bf6559418247?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
           </div> 
           <div id="comment-127354" class="comment-content"> 
            <span class="name">rasenplanscher <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
            <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127354"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T09:24:08+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 9:24 am</time></a> </span> 
            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>Have you actually looked in a `node_modules` directory? The way it works, you get the same piece of code many times (possibly different versions of it) – there is no lesser code footprint. If anything, it increases. The benefit lies in not re-inventing the wheel, while the price is actually *more* code.</p> 
            <div class="reply"> 
             <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127354#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127354&quot;, &quot;127354&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to rasenplanscher">Reply</a>

         <li class="comment even depth-2" id="li-comment-127364"> <a name="comment-127364"></a> 
          <div class="comment-container"> 
           <div class="avatar">
            <img alt="" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/11921529910d7b94a2f17fa8b297abbf?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/11921529910d7b94a2f17fa8b297abbf?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
           </div> 
           <div id="comment-127364" class="comment-content"> 
            <span class="name">Alex-PK <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
            <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127364"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T10:21:02+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 10:21 am</time></a> </span> 
            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>This is not entirely true with npm.</p> 
            <p>Each package you depend on could depend on a different version of another package, and npm will happily download it multiple times, and when you package it for the client, it will be repeated n times in the bundle.</p> 
            <p>If npm did proper dependency graph check, js developer would be forced to respect semver, for example, and to maintain their packages or risk conflicting with others and lose karma.</p> 
            <p>This would improve the package ecosystem a lot, IMO.</p> 
            <div class="reply"> 
             <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127364#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127364&quot;, &quot;127364&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Alex-PK">Reply</a>

       <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-127230"> <a name="comment-127230"></a> 
        <div class="comment-container"> 
         <div class="avatar">
          <img alt="" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/d47d0397605e4b9cab9fd8fce027e3b5?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/d47d0397605e4b9cab9fd8fce027e3b5?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">

         <div id="comment-127230" class="comment-content"> 
          <span class="name">Ian <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
          <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127230"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T19:39:30+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 7:39 pm</time></a> </span> 
          <span class="edit"></span> 
          <p>In a lot of Javascript environments, space is at a premium. Downloading that 150kb trigonometry library has just worsened your page response time. Several larger libraries like Underscore (and Lodash) have actually intentionally split themselves into sub-modules because people usually only ever load them to use a single merge function.</p> 
          <p>Rewriting these sorts of things is totally possible, but each time you do you’re violating the DRY principle. The issue here isn’t how large or small a dependency is, it’s how to cope with churn in your dependency tree. A large module could just as easily been removed from NPM and the same problem would have occurred. There are solutions that exist to freeze, localize and otherwise harden dependency modules, but none are considered standard yet. This, most likely, will bring them to the forefront.</p> 
          <div class="reply"> 
           <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127230#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127230&quot;, &quot;127230&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Ian">Reply</a> 
          </div>
          <!-- /.reply --> 
         </div>

           <li class="comment even depth-3" id="li-comment-127351"> <a name="comment-127351"></a> 
            <div class="comment-container"> 
             <div class="avatar">
              <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/cf73f3e29f953283ec8c5f7219a1e094?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/cf73f3e29f953283ec8c5f7219a1e094?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
             </div> 
             <div id="comment-127351" class="comment-content"> 
              <span class="name">Tom <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
              <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127351"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T08:23:35+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 8:23 am</time></a> </span> 
              <span class="edit"></span> 
              <p>No, front end tooling such as webpack that makes use of npm works the other way around. Rather than trim what isn’t used, start with lots of small modules and build them into only what is needed. This is why small, reusable modules are a very good thing for client side development. Sometimes these are modules you have written yourself and sometimes they are third party dependencies which IMO should be limited to those that you can absolutely trust.</p> 
              <p>Also I think you misunderstand. The small modules are not deployed as small modules that each gets loaded as an independent asset. I agree that would be madness. They are built into a single package – or multiple chunks that can then be lazy-loaded at the appropriate point.</p> 
              <p>Think of npm modules as small, reusable building blocks. The whole point of npm and modularisation is for things to be small – and yes that sometimes means a module being a single function makes absolute sense.</p>

               <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127351#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127351&quot;, &quot;127351&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Tom">Reply</a>

           <li class="comment even depth-3" id="li-comment-127373"> <a name="comment-127373"></a> 
            <div class="comment-container"> 
             <div class="avatar">
              <img alt="" src="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/836f2e9873e81dadef104376e52207d2?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/836f2e9873e81dadef104376e52207d2?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
             </div> 
             <div id="comment-127373" class="comment-content"> 
              <span class="name">Samuel Reed <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
              <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127373"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T10:53:04+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 10:53 am</time></a> </span> 
              <span class="edit"></span> 
              <p>There is – now. But there wasn’t a very short time ago, and many packages still need to change their internal structure to support it.</p> 
              <p>Rollup &amp; Webpack 2 can do tree shaking (dead code elimination… but actually more like live code identification) because of the static nature of ES6 modules. The previous CJS (`require()`) convention made it very difficult to do this effectively.</p> 
              <p>When ES6 modules become more mainstream, I think we will see a larger death of these simple modules in favor of large utility libraries like lodash. It will be perfectly reasonable to install lodash just to include something like `_.padStart()`, because:</p> 
              <p>1. It might be included elsewhere in the project, so installation cost is literally 0, and<br> 2. For browser bundling, the 40kb+ module can be reduced to &lt; 1kb as unused code is removed.</p> 
              <p>This will go a long way toward restoring sanity.</p> 
              <div class="reply"> 
               <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127373#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127373&quot;, &quot;127373&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Samuel Reed">Reply</a> 
              </div>

         <!-- #comment-## --> 
         <li class="comment odd alt depth-2" id="li-comment-127349"> <a name="comment-127349"></a> 
          <div class="comment-container"> 
           <div class="avatar">
            <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/cf73f3e29f953283ec8c5f7219a1e094?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/cf73f3e29f953283ec8c5f7219a1e094?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
           </div> 
           <div id="comment-127349" class="comment-content"> 
            <span class="name">Tom <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
            <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127349"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T08:10:46+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 8:10 am</time></a> </span> 
            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>This absolutely! What everyone seems to be forgetting is that we are talking about client-side code. It’s in our interests to make it as small as possible. So you can’t compare it to .NET for example where it’s perfectly fine to include an entire trigonometry library even if you end up using only a single method.</p> 
            <p>There is nothing wrong with small modules as dependencies e.g. the lodash modules. They may independently do relatively simple things that yes you could write yourself, but as a whole they are a well used, well maintained, well tested, well documented set of core modules, so if you need more than one or two functions, then why not use them? It doesn’t make you a bad programmer to do so. </p> 
            <p>Small modules allow tooling such as webpack to optimise the built size of your deployed JavaScript assets. They are generally a good thing.</p> 
            <p>Size is not the problem here. What is wrong, is being dependency happy and using any old module. You have to be able to trust the source of your dependency. Does it have good tests? Does it have an active user base? And I agree that perhaps npm needs to make it harder for much depended-on modules to simply be removed without a very good reason. If something was published as open source to npm then perhaps npm needs to take ownership of it.</p> 
            <div class="reply"> 
             <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127349#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127349&quot;, &quot;127349&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Tom">Reply</a> 
            </div>

       <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-127231"> <a name="comment-127231"></a> 
        <div class="comment-container"> 
         <div class="avatar">
          <img alt="" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/1162003d91a663aaae4f31b5b4743cdd?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/1162003d91a663aaae4f31b5b4743cdd?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
         </div> 
         <div id="comment-127231" class="comment-content"> 
          <span class="name">David Arsenault <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
          <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127231"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T19:43:02+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 7:43 pm</time></a> </span> 
          <span class="edit"></span> 
          <p>Amen. Very well said! This trend is actually insane. Dependencies should be used WHEN NECESSARY not just because you can find a one line or one function package. What you suggest here is very reasonable indeed. I integrate small functions from across other libs in my current C dev work (high perf computing) rather that take on dependencies. Dependencies represent several flavors of risk: risk of external code breaking me, tail risk for code maintenance, unknown performance risk (what’s that code do inside?), and messy mental model risk for understanding code with different APIs, naming, etc.</p> 
          <div class="reply"> 
           <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127231#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127231&quot;, &quot;127231&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to David Arsenault">Reply</a>

            <span class="name"><a href="http://crubier.net" rel="external nofollow" class="url">Crubier</a> <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
            <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127341"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T07:37:37+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 7:37 am</time></a> </span> 
            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>Have you ever heard of DRY ?</p> 
            <p>Why should EVERY JS programmer bake their very own version of widely used functions, when battle-tested implementations are available using ONE npm install command ?</p> 
            <p>This trend is actually the very thing that causes the success of the JS ecosystem, whether you like it or not. If done well, dependencies do not create the risks you are talking about. These risks are just the result of poor dependency management.</p>

       <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-127232"> <a name="comment-127232"></a> 
        <div class="comment-container"> 
         <div class="avatar">
          <img alt="" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a63dce67a9b666a6bbbd067e25360abe?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a63dce67a9b666a6bbbd067e25360abe?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
         </div> 
         <div id="comment-127232" class="comment-content"> 
          <span class="name">Freddy Rangel <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
          <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127232"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T19:52:47+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 7:52 pm</time></a> </span> 
          <span class="edit"></span> 
          <p>I call this kind of attitude the “Tea Party” of JavaScript development. The reason why we currently have JavaScript tooling fatigue is exactly because Tea Party developers insist on writing everything themselves instead of trying to build a better abstraction. The lesson here isn’t not fewer dependencies: it’s managing dependencies. NPM should not allow someone to arbitrarily remove modules that other’s may be depending on. It’s like building a bridge and them deciding to remove it after a whole city now depends on it.</p> 
          <div class="reply"> 
           <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127232#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127232&quot;, &quot;127232&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Freddy Rangel">Reply</a>

       <li class="comment odd alt thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-127233"> <a name="comment-127233"></a> 
        <div class="comment-container"> 
         <div class="avatar">
          <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/0d100cc4ad5735c76c7be62e9bb76929?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/0d100cc4ad5735c76c7be62e9bb76929?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
         </div> 
         <div id="comment-127233" class="comment-content"> 
          <span class="name">Jamie <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
          <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127233"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T19:59:01+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 7:59 pm</time></a> </span> 
          <span class="edit"></span> 
          <p>We haven’t forgotten how to program. We’ve just got better tools for sharing things we use a lot. You see one 11 line function. I see one of hundreds of such things that I will need in lots of different projects, and prefer not to reinvent, unit test, and maintain independently in each place I use it.</p> 
          <p>Once you’ve decided to take code reuse and versioning seriously, instead of just having an organic, unique junk drawer that grows in each project, with untested edge cases lurking around every corner, and each project havibg slightly different implementations of nearly identical tools, you will realize that this is actually very sensible. We just never had a package manager that was so lightweight it didn’t get in your way, and it was actually worth creating a package so small.</p> 
          <div class="reply"> 
           <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127233#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127233&quot;, &quot;127233&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Jamie">Reply</a>

          <p>.. Also this has nothing to do with creating small footprints. NPM was created for node, it’s adoption for use in client work is very recent. It’s philosophical. If it was invisible, why wouldn’t you want to treat to every thing with no actual coupling to something else as a unique entity?           <span class="edit"></span> 
          <p>Small re-usable components are exactly the point with npmjs.</p> 
          <p>JavaScript may be lacking when compared to other “standardised” languages with large footprints, but that is part of the charm of being able to build up your app using the dependencies you need. I think Sindre put this quite eloquently in his “post” on the topic. </p> 
          <p>But I do agree with you on being responsible with your dependencies. Add and use them deliberately based on your context.</p>

          <span class="edit"></span> 
          <p>“In every other language I have ever used, string padding is either built-in, or it’s part of a standard runtime that’s available locally on every workstation and build server. But not in JavaScript – if you want to pad a string in JS, you either write your own function, you copy &amp; paste one from StackOverflow, or you import a package to do it for you, and based on the fallout from yesterday it looks like a lot of people went for the package option.”</p>

       <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-127243"> <a name="comment-127243"></a> 
        <div class="comment-container"> 
         <div class="avatar">
          <img alt="" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a3933f84673e75f5887c5cc7e25f17a0?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a3933f84673e75f5887c5cc7e25f17a0?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
         </div> 
         <div id="comment-127243" class="comment-content"> 
          <span class="name">Eric <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
          <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127243"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T20:27:44+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 8:27 pm</time></a> </span> 
          <span class="edit"></span> 
          <p>Why would you want to install a 500kb dependency that has only one function you need, when you can install a 10kb dependency that has it?</p> 
          <p>Would you want each of your five 20kb dependencies to re-implement the same 5kb function, increasing the code you must send to the client by 20%, or would it be more optimal for each of those dependency to use the same 5kb function?</p> 
          <p>The author comes from rants about practices of developers from different programming environment, without experience, without figuring how things came to be in the first place. If he did give an effort to think from the perspective from Node.JS developers he’d have addressed the previous two points. </p> 
          <p>This is like going to a friend’s house and complaining everything is put in the wrong place. It would have been wise to immerse in Node.JS conventions and observe for a while before making comment.</p>

       <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-127244"> <a name="comment-127244"></a> 
        <div class="comment-container"> 
         <div class="avatar">
          <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/0d100cc4ad5735c76c7be62e9bb76929?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/0d100cc4ad5735c76c7be62e9bb76929?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
         </div> 
         <div id="comment-127244" class="comment-content"> 
          <span class="name">Jamie <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
          <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127244"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-23T20:33:01+00:00">March 23, 2016 at 8:33 pm</time></a> </span> 
          <span class="edit"></span> 
          <p>One final observation about this fray. In the aftermath a hundred people posted one liners and said see? Why would you use some package instead of just inlining this simple code. </p> 
          <p>Yet of all the ones I saw come across Twitter, every one failed the unit test in the silly 11 line module. Because they forgot about common use cases such as non-string operands or omitted the default spacing character.</p> 
          <p>Even 11 line functions need to be tested. Even 11 line functions have non trivial features (such as an optional parameter) that you might want. Now you’ve got an API that you need to replicate everywhere you reinvent this thing, or confuse your developers.</p> 
          <p>Why decide to not use practices that apply to your major work – testing, consistency, reuse – to a single function? Just because? The only argument seems to be “because I can easily rewrite it”. </p> 
          <p>Almost all substantive code is composed of a lot of things that you could easily rewrite. How much more productive can you be if you don’t have to rewrite all that every time?</p> 
          <div class="reply"> 
           <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127244#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127244&quot;, &quot;127244&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Jamie">Reply</a> 
          </div>

         <li class="comment odd alt depth-2" id="li-comment-127353"> <a name="comment-127353"></a> 
          <div class="comment-container"> 
           <div class="avatar">
            <img alt="" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/15f111b4e413a12de6dd05745e674199?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/15f111b4e413a12de6dd05745e674199?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
           </div> 
           <div id="comment-127353" class="comment-content"> 
            <span class="name">Anonymous Coder <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
            <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127353"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T09:13:06+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 9:13 am</time></a> </span> 
            <span class="edit"></span> 
            <p>Fine, here you go:</p> 
            <p>function leftpad (str, len, ch=’ ‘) {<br> return Array(len-String(str).length+1).join(String(ch))+str;<br> }</p> 
            <p>That passes all the unit tests contained in that package.</p> 
            <p>Understand that it is legitimate to complain about how bad a piece of code is (and face facts, that leftpad code he has is pretty bad), especially when it’s used by so many projects. This sort of code-reuse is only good when the underlying code is good as well. Making bad code is a problem no matter how you slice it.</p> 
            <div class="reply"> 
             <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127353#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127353&quot;, &quot;127353&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Anonymous Coder">Reply</a>

           <li class="comment even depth-3" id="li-comment-127386"> <a name="comment-127386"></a> 
            <div class="comment-container"> 
             <div class="avatar">
              <img alt="" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/0d100cc4ad5735c76c7be62e9bb76929?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/0d100cc4ad5735c76c7be62e9bb76929?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
             </div> 
             <div id="comment-127386" class="comment-content"> 
              <span class="name">Jamie <span class="says">says :</span></span> 
              <span class="date"> <a href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/#comment-127386"><time pubdate datetime="2016-03-24T12:19:22+00:00">March 24, 2016 at 12:19 pm</time></a> </span> 
              <span class="edit"></span> 
              <p>The point isn’t that *you can’t write a working implementation*. Of course you can. This is about sharing code, not about whether you happen to like the code he wrote.</p> 
              <p>If you want to publish your arguably better implementation and publish it under @anonymouscoder/leftpad then I would applaud you for embracing code reuse.</p> 
              <p>And since it’s API compatible with “left-pad”, it’s really easy for me to start using your new, better implementation if I want to! Everyone wins. (Technically, though, your version is not the same: if I pass it an object, it crashes, instead of converting it to [Object object]. But since that wasn’t unit tested in the original, I’ll give you a break).</p> 
              <p>None of this would be possible if you just inlined your own version of everything though. If one day you realize you need “left-pad” to be resilient when passed an object, you can’t just update it in one place and be done. You’ve probably got dozens of different versions floating around in all your projects by that time.</p> 
              <div class="reply"> 
               <a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="http://www.haneycodes.net/npm-left-pad-have-we-forgotten-how-to-program/?replytocom=127386#respond" onclick="return addComment.moveForm( &quot;comment-127386&quot;, &quot;127386&quot;, &quot;respond&quot;, &quot;824&quot; )" aria-label="Reply to Jamie">Reply</a>

       <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-127246"> <a name="comment-127246"></a> 
        <div class="comment-container"> 
         <div class="avatar">
          <img alt="" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/4c92dc41019b1eb7f5ce8dad3e43a78c?s=80&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/4c92dc41019b1eb7f5ce8dad3e43a78c?s=160&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x" class="avatar avatar-80 photo" height="80" width="80">
